<p><i><b>Unorthodox Jukebox</b></i> is the second studio album by American singer and songwriter <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Mars" title="Bruno Mars">Bruno Mars</a>. It was released on December 7, 2012, by <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Records" title="Atlantic Records">Atlantic Records</a>. It serves as the follow-up to Mars' debut record <i><a href="/wiki/Doo-Wops_%26_Hooligans" title="Doo-Wops &amp; Hooligans">Doo-Wops &amp; Hooligans</a></i> (2010). The album was initially planned to be more "energetic" than his previous work, but, it ended presenting a wide range of styles such as <a href="/wiki/Reggae_rock" title="Reggae rock">reggae rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disco" title="Disco">disco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul music</a>. The singer co-wrote the whole record and worked with several past collaborators, while enlisting new producers and no guest vocalists. Lyrically, <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> revolves around the theme of relationships, incorporating more explicit lyrics and subjects than his previous material. Critical response to <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was generally favorable; many <a href="/wiki/Music_journalism" title="Music journalism">reviewers</a> compared Mars' work to that of his previous album, while others deemed the lyrical content as shallow and lacking of depth.</p>
<p>On December 4, 2012, the album was made available to listen to in its entirety for a week before its release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The album debuted at number 2 on the US <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>, with first week sales of 192,000 copies, becoming Mars' best opening week to date. <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> later topped the chart, marking the singer's first album to do so. The record further peaked at number one in Australia, Canada, Switzerland, and the United Kingdom. <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> became Mars' fastest-selling album in the latter country and its third in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MusicWeek_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-MusicWeek-4">[4]</a></sup> the third best-selling album in Australia in 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARIA_2013_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARIA_2013-5">[5]</a></sup> and the fifth in the United States in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_the_Phonographic_Industry" title="International Federation of the Phonographic Industry">International Federation of the Phonographic Industry</a> (IFPI) reported that <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was globally the fourth best-selling record in 2013, with 3.2 million copies, and 6 million units as of January 2015. The record won the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Pop_Vocal_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Pop Vocal Album">Grammy Award for Best Pop Vocal Album</a> at the <a href="/wiki/56th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="56th Annual Grammy Awards">56th Annual Grammy Awards</a>.</p>
<p>The premiere of the album was preceded by the release of its lead single, "<a href="/wiki/Locked_Out_of_Heaven" title="Locked Out of Heaven">Locked Out of Heaven</a>", which experienced commercial acclaim worldwide and topped the US <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> chart for six consecutive weeks. Meanwhile, promotional singles "<a href="/wiki/Young_Girls" title="Young Girls">Young Girls</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Moonshine_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Moonshine (Bruno Mars song)">Moonshine</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/When_I_Was_Your_Man" title="When I Was Your Man">When I Was Your Man</a>" were released in November and December 2013. Subsequently, four other singles ("When I Was Your Man", "<a href="/wiki/Treasure_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Treasure (Bruno Mars song)">Treasure</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Gorilla_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Gorilla (Bruno Mars song)">Gorilla</a>" and "Young Girls") were made available for consumption, with each one of them having major to minor success in the United States. <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was further promoted through the <a href="/wiki/Moonshine_Jungle_Tour" title="Moonshine Jungle Tour">Moonshine Jungle Tour</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conception">Conception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Conception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After ending <a href="/wiki/The_Doo-Wops_%26_Hooligans_Tour" title="The Doo-Wops &amp; Hooligans Tour">The Doo-Wops &amp; Hooligans Tour</a>, Mars implied that he was going to take some time creating and perfecting his second full-length album. He additionally confessed that, "It's going to come when it comes" since his production team, The Smeezingtons, felt that they rushed his debut album because of the release's deadline. "We just want it to be perfect", the singer added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruno_Mars_and_Smeezingtons_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruno_Mars_and_Smeezingtons-7">[7]</a></sup> Due to the numerous television shows and worldwide performances provided by Mars, he acknowledged that his second studio album needed to display his "raucously" dynamic appearances on stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardcover_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-billboardcover-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mars revealed to <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> in September 2012 on a preview of his cover story that he recorded an album that represented his freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unorthodox_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-unorthodox-9">[9]</a></sup> On his debut record, <i><a href="/wiki/Doo-Wops_%26_Hooligans" title="Doo-Wops &amp; Hooligans">Doo-Wops &amp; Hooligans</a></i>, the singer reportedly had to change some things because of pressure from his label, something he was displeased with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardcover_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-billboardcover-8">[8]</a></sup> He clarified that <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was more of him and what he stands for, mainly because he didn't have to modify things. He elaborated, "If I'm changing things around because people might think it's a hard pill to swallow then I'm going to feel like a circus clown onstage, selling something fake".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardcover_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-billboardcover-8">[8]</a></sup> The variety of music styles presented on <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> resulted in his rejection from his label several times in the past; he also confessed that he had to face criticism by label presidents, who—according to him—expressed, "Your music sucks, you don't know who you are, your music is all over the place, and we don't know how to market this stuff. Pick a lane and come back to us". To these statements, the singer added that he felt "disgusted" as he wanted to have the liberty to create his own music and not the music he was told to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardcover_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-billboardcover-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mars further confessed that by the time he started to develop his songwriting skills, he learned his "most valuable lesson", "Does it make you move? Make you dance? Whether the song is uptempo or a ballad", adding that "there has to be a heartbeat in back of it. There needs to be a pulse in the song".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_artist_of_the_year_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_artist_of_the_year-10">[10]</a></sup> To <i><a href="/wiki/American_Songwriter" title="American Songwriter">American Songwriter</a></i>, Philip Lawrence from The Smeezingtons recalled sleepless nights during the record's process, as the team was trying to prove that the debut album "wasn't a fluke", which according to Lawrence is "the absolute wrong mind-set to be". He went on expressing that the first four or five months they worked on the record were fruitless, because "nothing would stick". When they decided to leave the studio and have a few drinks, they came to conclusion to relax and not put themselves under so much pressure; it resulted in "the ideas [starting] coming out again."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruno_Mars.E2.80.99_Right_Hand_Man_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruno_Mars.E2.80.99_Right_Hand_Man-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recording">Recording</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Recording">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div style="margin:0px 2em;">
<p>The first thing he said was, 'I want to sound exactly the opposite of what a Mark Ronson collaboration with Bruno Mars is supposed to sound like.' That won me over – then I found out what a phenomenal talent he is. This is the most progressive music I've worked on yet. It's going to open up the arteries and change the sound of music".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
</div>
<div style="position: absolute; left: 0px; top: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">“</div>
<div style="position: absolute; right: 0px; bottom: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">”</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<a href="/wiki/Mark_Ronson" title="Mark Ronson">Mark Ronson</a> on working with Bruno Mars.</cite></div>
<p>Recording took place at Larrabee Sound Studios in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, Levcon Studios in <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">Hollywood</a>, Daptone Studios in <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Avatar_Studios" title="Avatar Studios">Avatar Studios</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liner_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-liner-13">[13]</a></sup> Having worked with record producer and multi-instrumentalist <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bhasker" title="Jeff Bhasker">Jeff Bhasker</a> on <i>Doo-Wops &amp; Hooligans</i>, Mars enlisted him and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Ronson" title="Mark Ronson">Mark Ronson</a> as he wanted the record to incorporate live instrumentation, but to still be suitable for clubs; Bhasker particularly admitted that it sounded great on the radio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup> American disc jockey <a href="/wiki/Diplo" title="Diplo">Diplo</a> was able to further push the album from the "safety net" with something for the [strip] club on "Money Makes Her Smile"; Mars claimed that the DJ could make the club go "wild" due to the sounds on his computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The development of <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> started when Mars wanted to create something unforeseen with the follow-up of his debut album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unorthodox_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-unorthodox-9">[9]</a></sup> He began not only by not allowing himself to "get boxed in to any one genre" during the recording sessions for the album, but also by showing the influences of several other music styles "by having the autonomy and luxury of walking into the studio and claim [he feels] like doing a hip-hop, R&amp;B, soul or rock record". Due to the music variety, the singer refused to "pick a lane" for <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unorthodox_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-unorthodox-9">[9]</a></sup> As Mars had a lot of time to create the album, something that didn't happen during his debut,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruno_Mars.E2.80.99_Right_Hand_Man_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruno_Mars.E2.80.99_Right_Hand_Man-11">[11]</a></sup> he reportedly had the possibility to develop a musically outstanding product that displayed his music taste and completely revealed his artistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardcover_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-billboardcover-8">[8]</a></sup> Because he had been a composer of pop and radio-friendly songs for a long time, it ultimately influenced the style of his debut; however, he didn't have the chance to craft the "sounds and sonics" he wanted to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardcover_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-billboardcover-8">[8]</a></sup> As a result, Mars hoped to "let loose" and discuss darker, more risqué subject matter, while drawing on the "danger" embraced by pop artists such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prince_(musician)" title="Prince (musician)">Prince</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dream_team_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-dream_team-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avatar-studios.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2432" data-file-width="2650" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Avatar-studios.jpg/170px-Avatar-studios.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Avatar-studios.jpg/255px-Avatar-studios.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Avatar-studios.jpg/340px-Avatar-studios.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avatar-studios.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Avatar_Studios" title="Avatar Studios">Avatar Studios</a>, one of the four studios where the album was recorded.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To explore and create a new sound, Mars enlisted the help of Mark Ronson, Jeff Bhasker and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Epworth" title="Paul Epworth">Paul Epworth</a>. The singer described this encounter as inviting "master chefs into the kitchen with no master plan" or any kind of outcome, "either a complete disaster or something outstanding".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dream_team_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-dream_team-14">[14]</a></sup> Previously, <a href="/wiki/Benny_Blanco" title="Benny Blanco">Benny Blanco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emile_Haynie" title="Emile Haynie">Emile Haynie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diplo" title="Diplo">Diplo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supa_Dups" title="Supa Dups">Supa Dups</a>, as well as Mars' production team <a href="/wiki/The_Smeezingtons" title="The Smeezingtons">The Smeezingtons</a>, have been confirmed to have worked on the album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unorthodox_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-unorthodox-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nina_Simone_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nina_Simone-15">[15]</a></sup> The first song written for <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was "Gorilla", which "set the tone for the entire project"; Mars explained in an interview granted to <a href="/wiki/MTV_News" title="MTV News">MTV News</a> that it became its mascot hence the reason it ended up as the album cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YG_Video_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-YG_Video-16">[16]</a></sup> In an interview for the <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i>, Blanco confessed, "I got a really cool song with him. Me and Paul [Epworth] just got together and Bruno wrote an amazing song on top of it. It kind of all came together". He further explained that "it's like some throwback <a href="/wiki/Nina_Simone" title="Nina Simone">Nina Simone</a> type shit, like 'Sinnerman'". Additionally, a "piano-laced" track was conceived for the album in its opening session.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nina_Simone_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nina_Simone-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mars explained the meaning behind the album's title by saying that it had become a "soulful, experimental, electronic, hard-to-explain" concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup> In order to record an album like that, it was—according to him—mandatory to experiment and not follow any known pattern, resulting the guests enlisted for the record to be unexpected. According to the singer, jazz artist <a href="/wiki/Esperanza_Spalding" title="Esperanza Spalding">Esperanza Spalding</a> and Diplo were among them. Furthermore, the "pop's most innovative producers" (Bhasker, Haynie and Ronson) had to be taken "beyond their comfort zone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup> While being interviewed for his <i>Billboard Artist of the Year</i> cover story, Mars complimented Ronson and Bhasker by explaining that "it's not about what's hot on the radio or the fastest way to make a buck, these guys are fearless, doing the music they want to do".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_artist_of_the_year_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_artist_of_the_year-10">[10]</a></sup> Mars additionally felt that "when there are no safe bets, that's when [he feels his] blood move."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of September 2012, the singer was reportedly "fine-tuning" the mixing of a track until 5 a.m. with Manny Marroquin, mainly because of his disappointment with "<a href="/wiki/It_Will_Rain" title="It Will Rain">It Will Rain</a>"<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s final mix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardcover_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-billboardcover-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unorthodox_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-unorthodox-9">[9]</a></sup> Starting with mid-October, The Smeezingtons were making the final touches on the album at Levcon Studios (their studio); Ari Levine sent a few tracks over e-mail with their final mixes to Mars, who was still in Manhattan after hosting Saturday Night Live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dream_team_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-dream_team-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The album opens with "<a href="/wiki/Young_Girls" title="Young Girls">Young Girls</a>", a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midtempo" title="Midtempo">midtempo</a> <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> ballad which deals with the idea of indulging in the dubious charms of young girls, even though the singer recognizes that what he is doing is wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_artist_of_the_year_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_artist_of_the_year-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hitfix_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-hitfix-17">[17]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Locked_Out_of_Heaven" title="Locked Out of Heaven">Locked Out of Heaven</a>", a <a href="/wiki/Reggae_rock" title="Reggae rock">reggae rock</a> track that incorporates elements of <a href="/wiki/New_wave_music" title="New wave music">new wave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">funk</a>, is the subsequent track and the lead single.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maerz_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Maerz-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glamour_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-glamour-18">[18]</a></sup> Musically, it contains booming synthesizers, a <a href="/wiki/Four_on_the_floor_(music)" title="Four on the floor (music)">four-on-the-floor</a> chorus, while lyrically exploring themes of love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loohreview_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-loohreview-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTV_LOOH_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTV_LOOH-20">[20]</a></sup> Comparisons were established between "Locked Out of Heaven" and the rock/reggae style used by English band <a href="/wiki/The_Police" title="The Police">The Police</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loohreview_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-loohreview-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sendra_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-22">[22]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Gorilla_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Gorilla (Bruno Mars song)">Gorilla</a>" was described by Mars as being about "good old animalistic sex".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dream_team_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-dream_team-14">[14]</a></sup> The track portrays a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midtempo" title="Midtempo">midtempo</a> <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soft_rock" title="Soft rock">soft rock</a> song,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_artist_of_the_year_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_artist_of_the_year-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Empire_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Empire-23">[23]</a></sup> while lyrically expressing male <a href="/wiki/Chauvinism" title="Chauvinism">chauvinist</a> sentiments<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chan_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chan-24">[24]</a></sup> and making reference to his 2010 arrest for <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> possession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Treasure_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Treasure (Bruno Mars song)">Treasure</a>", the album's third single, is a disco-pop song, inspired by English duo <a href="/wiki/Wham!" title="Wham!">Wham!</a> and "Baby I'm Yours" (2010) released by French disc jockey <a href="/wiki/Breakbot" title="Breakbot">Breakbot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The latter similarities led the song to be re-registered in <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Composers,_Authors_and_Publishers" title="American Society of Composers, Authors and Publishers">ASCAP</a> with new writing credits, which included <a href="/wiki/Breakbot" title="Breakbot">Thibaut Berland</a> and Irfane Christopher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Moonshine_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Moonshine (Bruno Mars song)">Moonshine</a>", the second promotional single from the record, portrays a recording with ample influences of disco, <a href="/wiki/Power_pop" title="Power pop">power pop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quiet_storm" title="Quiet storm">quiet storm</a>, while being compared to the work done by French <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic</a> duo <a href="/wiki/Daft_Punk" title="Daft Punk">Daft Punk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dream_team_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-dream_team-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chan_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chan-24">[24]</a></sup> Particularly, Chris Martins of <i><a href="/wiki/Spin_(magazine)" title="Spin (magazine)">Spin</a></i> wrote that the song "channels the King of Pop, Michael Jackson with a markedly more reverent aplomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The song "<a href="/wiki/When_I_Was_Your_Man" title="When I Was Your Man">When I Was Your Man</a>" is a <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> <a href="/wiki/Piano_ballad" title="Piano ballad">piano ballad</a> and shows traditional notions of romance, a pre-fame heartbreak as Mars regrets letting his woman get away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chan_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chan-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Melinda Newman of <i><a href="/wiki/HitFix" title="HitFix">HitFix</a></i> thought the track "sounds like a cross between <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">Stevie Wonder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a>," also seeing "a touch of Michael Jackson" in his delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hitfix_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-hitfix-17">[17]</a></sup> Andy Gill of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> called it a "<a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">McCartney</a>-esque piano ballad",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill-31">[31]</a></sup> while Jason Lipshut of <i>Billboard</i> wrote that "it will make for a killer lighters-in-the-air moment in concert. Although it's not quite an <a href="/wiki/Alicia_Keys" title="Alicia Keys">Alicia Keys</a>-esque powerhouse, [it] smartly allows Mars to momentarily remove his fedora and bare his soul".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBR_UJ_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBR_UJ-32">[32]</a></sup> The single was additionally likened to the material of Wham!.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price-26">[26]</a></sup> With "Natalie", Mars exhibits further male chauvinist sentiments<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chan_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chan-24">[24]</a></sup> over a girl named Natalie which stole Mars' money and ran away with it; the singer is therefore plotting murderous revenge against her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sendra_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The song was described as "a sort of methed-up "<a href="/wiki/Dirty_Diana" title="Dirty Diana">Dirty Diana</a>", assembled with fierce handclaps and hard-edged oooh-oooh vocal echos".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup> Lipshutz called it the "flip-side of "When I Was Your Man" and pointed out Mars lamenting the hypnotism of a "gold-digging bitch". "Show Me" portrays a recording with ample influences of reggae<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sendra_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rytlewski_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rytlewski-34">[34]</a></sup> and dancehall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maerz_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Maerz-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup> These infusions are easily noticed by the "over sampled air horns",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maerz_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Maerz-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rytlewski_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rytlewski-34">[34]</a></sup> "tape-echo effects"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maerz_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Maerz-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup> and steel drums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ninth track from <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i>, "Money Makes Her Smile", is the result of a trip to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strip_clubs" title="Strip clubs">strip club</a> done by Mars and Diplo, who afterwards decided to create an "anthem" for it, since Mars' <a href="/wiki/Just_the_Way_You_Are_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Just the Way You Are (Bruno Mars song)">debut solo single</a> was an awful song to be played in such place, according to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup> The recording was described as a "strip-club-directed banger",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maerz_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-Maerz-1">[1]</a></sup> infused with disco elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobeReview_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobeReview-35">[35]</a></sup> Its composition includes "rapid-fire chants, breathless percussion, propulsive electronic blips"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBR_UJ_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBR_UJ-32">[32]</a></sup> along with "rave sirens and hardcore punk rattle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup> Lyrically, the song describes a gold digger who can only be satisfied by "fat stacks of money".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-8"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sendra_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-21">[21]</a></sup> The album's final song of the standard edition, "If I Knew", is a <a href="/wiki/Sam_Cooke" title="Sam Cooke">Sam Cooke</a>-inspired ballad<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sendra_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-21">[21]</a></sup> which incorporates soul<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gill-31">[31]</a></sup> and doo-wop nuances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBR_UJ_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-BBR_UJ-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reed-36">[36]</a></sup> It lyrically delves on themes of regret.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBR_UJ_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-BBR_UJ-32">[32]</a></sup> "Old &amp; Crazy", a duet between Mars and <a href="/wiki/Esperanza_Spalding" title="Esperanza Spalding">Esperanza Spalding</a>, was included on the deluxe version of <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-9"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Jeff Bhasker, one of the track's producers, confessed that the song is reminiscent of the sound of a 1920s Paris club. He went on explaining that its process began with Emile Haynie producing a beat and Bhasker adding a sample of a <a href="/wiki/Django_Reinhardt" title="Django Reinhardt">Django Reinhardt</a> recording over it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration_12-10"><a href="#cite_note-.27Unorthodox.27_Inspiration-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Singles">Singles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Singles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Locked_Out_of_Heaven" title="Locked Out of Heaven">Locked Out of Heaven</a>" was serviced to mainstream radio stations and was digitally released was on October 1, 2012 as the album's <a href="/wiki/Lead_single" title="Lead single">lead single</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTV_LOOH_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-MTV_LOOH-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllAccess_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllAccess-38">[38]</a></sup> It received positive commentary from music critics, who praised its reggae, rock and funky beat, but also its lyrics for talking about passion in a "tidy and impeccable" way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Commercially, the recording was a success, peaking atop the US <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a></i> and claiming the position for six weeks, marking Mars' longest-running number one on the chart after previously released "<a href="/wiki/Just_the_Way_You_Are_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Just the Way You Are (Bruno Mars song)">Just the Way You Are</a>" (2010) and "<a href="/wiki/Grenade_(song)" title="Grenade (song)">Grenade</a>" (2010) each topped the chart for four editions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The song further peaked at number one in Canada and on the US <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mainstream_Top_40_(Pop_Songs)" title="Mainstream Top 40 (Pop Songs)">Pop Songs</a> chart, while reaching the top ten in more than twenty countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruno_Mars_Hot_100_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruno_Mars_Hot_100-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/When_I_Was_Your_Man" title="When I Was Your Man">When I Was Your Man</a>" was released on January 15, 2013 as the album's second single.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FMQB_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FMQB-44">[44]</a></sup> It had previously been premiered as the album's third and final promotional single one month and a half prior to its stand-alone release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idolator_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-idolator-45">[45]</a></sup> The track was critically acclaimed, receiving praising for being an "emotional ballad" and for featuring a "minimal musical accompaniment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idolator_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-idolator-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popdust_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-popdust-46">[46]</a></sup> The song topped the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 chart, becoming the singer's fifth number one single in the United States;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> it reached the top fifteen in several fellow territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> "When I Was Your Man" was nominated for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Pop_Solo_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Pop Solo Performance">Best Pop Solo Performance</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/56th_Grammy_Awards" title="56th Grammy Awards">56th Grammy Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Treasure_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Treasure (Bruno Mars song)">Treasure</a>" was subsequently confirmed as the record's third single by <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Records" title="Atlantic Records">Atlantic Records</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> It received generally favorable reviews from critics, who praised it for "[echoing] the peppy sound of such pop/R&amp;B hit-makers of the 1970s and 1980s as the Sylvers, Heatwave, DeBarge and Kool &amp; the Gang."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> To promote the single, Mars performed on various occasions, including a "little extra Michael Jackson-esque, circa <i>Off The Wall</i> and <i>Thriller</i>" show at the 2013 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billboard_Music_Awards" title="Billboard Music Awards">Billboard Music Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Commercially, "Treasure" reached the top five in the United States and other eighteen countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruno_Mars_Hot_100_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruno_Mars_Hot_100-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Gorilla_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Gorilla (Bruno Mars song)">Gorilla</a>", confirmed as the next single for <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gorilla_MTV_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gorilla_MTV-54">[54]</a></sup> was produced by The Smeezingtons, Mark Ronson, Jeff Bhasker and Emile Haynie, who previously handled the process for the record's lead single.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liner_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-liner-13">[13]</a></sup> The track impacted American <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_hit_radio" title="Contemporary hit radio">pop radio</a> on September 10, 2013, thus peaking at number 22 on the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 and becoming the singer's first single not to reach the top ten there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruno_Mars_Hot_100_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bruno_Mars_Hot_100-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mainstream_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mainstream-55">[55]</a></sup> Mars performed the single for the first time during the <a href="/wiki/2013_MTV_Video_Music_Awards" title="2013 MTV Video Music Awards">2013 MTV Video Music Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gorilla_MTV_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gorilla_MTV-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Young_Girls" title="Young Girls">Young Girls</a>" was released as the fifth and final single from the album in selected countries, including Australia, North America and United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruno_Mars_calls_into_Smallzy_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruno_Mars_calls_into_Smallzy-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_Girls_release_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young_Girls_release-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Mars announced its availability during an interview with <a href="/wiki/Nova_96.9" title="Nova 96.9">Nova 96.9</a>, an Australian radio station, on November 26, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruno_Mars_calls_into_Smallzy_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruno_Mars_calls_into_Smallzy-56">[56]</a></sup> Commercially, its charting was influenced by its stand-alone release as the record's first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Promotional_single" title="Promotional single">promotional single</a> on November 6, 2012 through <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes Store</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YG_promo_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-YG_promo-59">[59]</a></sup> Unmaterialized plans for a music video to accompany "Young Girls" were made in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YG_Video_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-YG_Video-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release_and_promotion">Release and promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Release and promotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Moonshine_Jungle_Tour" title="Moonshine Jungle Tour">Moonshine Jungle Tour</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bruno_Mars_in_concert.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="1032" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bruno_Mars_in_concert.jpg/250px-Bruno_Mars_in_concert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bruno_Mars_in_concert.jpg/375px-Bruno_Mars_in_concert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bruno_Mars_in_concert.jpg/500px-Bruno_Mars_in_concert.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bruno_Mars_in_concert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mars and his band performing during the Moonshine Jungle Tour at Arena Ciudad de Mexico.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On September 4, 2012, Mars reported to his fanbase that, "You'll hear something in October. I promise", after being asked for news regarding his second studio album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> On September 19, 2012, the singer announced the release of <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> via <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> after having worked on it for a year. He further posted a link to a "Funny or Die" video entitled "Whatta Man", where Mars starred himself in order to celebrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> On September 28, 2012, <i>Billboard</i> reported the album's title and its American release date on December 11, 2012. In the same article, Mars revealed some of the guest producers, including Ronson, Bhasker, Diplo and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unorthodox_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-unorthodox-9">[9]</a></sup> He was as well featured on the week's cover story in which the track list was included;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unorthodox_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-unorthodox-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DS_UJT_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-DS_UJT-62">[62]</a></sup> it was also revealed by <a href="/wiki/Digital_Spy" title="Digital Spy">Digital Spy</a> the same day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DS_UJT_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-DS_UJT-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 24, 2012, the album's cover was unveiled by Mars after a promo shot previously used for a Billboard issue tried to pass as the official artwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ido_cover_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ido_cover-63">[63]</a></sup> On November 6, 2012, "Young Girls", the first promotional single from a series of three, was made available for consumption worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idolator_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-idolator-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YG_promo_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-YG_promo-59">[59]</a></sup> Subsequently, "<a href="/wiki/Moonshine_(Bruno_Mars_song)" title="Moonshine (Bruno Mars song)">Moonshine</a>" and "When I Was Your Man" were released as the last promotional singles on November 19, 2012 and December 3, 2012, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idolator_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-idolator-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mars performed "Locked Out of Heaven" and "Young Girls" for the first time on <i><a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Live" title="Saturday Night Live">Saturday Night Live</a></i> on October 20, 2012, while receiving positive reaction from critics and the audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Afterwards, he sang the same songs on November 7, 2012 at the 2012 <i><a href="/wiki/Victoria%27s_Secret_Fashion_Show" title="Victoria's Secret Fashion Show">Victoria's Secret Fashion Show</a></i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> the performance later aired on December 4, 2012 on <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> On November 24, 2012, Mars appeared on the <a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(UK_series_9)" title="The X Factor (UK series 9)">ninth season</a> of British <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(UK)" title="The X Factor (UK)">X Factor</a></i>, where he performed "Locked Out of Heaven".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> On December 8, 2012, Mars performed at the 2012 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Jingle_Bell_Ball" title="Jingle Bell Ball">Jingle Bell Ball</a></i>, an annually-held event promoted by <a href="/wiki/Capital_(radio_network)" title="Capital (radio network)">Capital FM</a>, which took place at the <a href="/wiki/The_O2_Arena" title="The O2 Arena">O2 Arena</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> whilst singing "Locked Out of Heaven" live on December 12, 2012 during the semi-finals of the <a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(U.S._season_2)" title="The X Factor (U.S. season 2)">second season</a> of American <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(U.S.)" title="The X Factor (U.S.)">X Factor</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Mars further performed the record's second single on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voice_(U.S._TV_series)" title="The Voice (U.S. TV series)">The Voice</a></i> during the final show of its <a href="/wiki/The_Voice_(U.S._season_3)" title="The Voice (U.S. season 3)">third season</a> held on December 17, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The singer embarked on the <a href="/wiki/Moonshine_Jungle_Tour" title="Moonshine Jungle Tour">Moonshine Jungle Tour</a> starting with June 22, 2013 at the <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Center" title="Verizon Center">Verizon Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tour_1_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tour_1-74">[74]</a></sup> The first shows of the tour also featured performances in Canada and Puerto Rico until September. Its second leg, announced on February 20, 2013, consisted of concerts all over Europe; it ran from October to November 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tour_1_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tour_1-74">[74]</a></sup> The Moonshine Jungle Tour ended on October 18, 2014, having Mars performing on a total of five legs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tour_1_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tour_1-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Slant_Magazine" title="Slant Magazine">Slant Magazine</a></td>

src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/17px-Star_full.svg.png 1.5x, 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/22px-Star_empty.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chan_24-4"><a <tr>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle"><i><a href="/wiki/Spin_(magazine)" title="Spin (magazine)">Spin</a></i></td>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle">8/10<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-7"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was met with generally favorable reviews from <a href="/wiki/Music_journalism" title="Music journalism">music critics</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>, which assigns a <a href="/wiki/Standard_score" title="Standard score">normalized</a> rating out of 100 reviews from mainstream critics, the album received an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weighted_mean" title="Weighted mean">average</a> score of 70, based on 16 reviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MC_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-MC-78">[78]</a></sup> Melissa Maerz from <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> gave the album an A– and stated that "[Mars'] talent for crafting little pop perfections of all stripes is undeniable", describing the record as a fusion of pop and R&amp;B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maerz_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-Maerz-1">[1]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine's <a href="/wiki/Jody_Rosen" title="Jody Rosen">Jody Rosen</a> awarded <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> four out of five stars, writing that, "The result is a record that makes the competition sound sad and idea-starved by comparison".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosen_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen-80">[80]</a></sup> Dan Hyman from <i><a href="/wiki/Spin_(magazine)" title="Spin (magazine)">Spin</a></i> granted the record an 8/10 rating, pointing out that "the bulk of <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> benefits from presenting [the singer] as he truly imagines himself: a big belter with an ear for pop hooks, sure, but one unafraid to dive into murkier waters." Hyman went on feeling that Mars' lyrics "get a desperately needed kick in the pants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyman_25-8"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-25">[25]</a></sup> Matt Cibula of <a href="/wiki/PopMatters" title="PopMatters">PopMatters</a> complimented the songs' <a href="/wiki/Hook_(music)" title="Hook (music)">hooks</a> and found the album "sung and arranged just as perfectly as his earlier work [...] a truly accomplished and slick pop album".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cibula_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cibula-79">[79]</a></sup> Jon Caramanica of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> labelled the songwriting exceptional, while praising Mars for being "a model of concision who always knows where the trigger is, and always, always pulls it". He went on complimenting the songs on the record, "these are some of the most energy-infused but profoundly cosseted songs you’re likely to hear all year".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caramanica_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caramanica-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> magazine's Jason Lipshutz described that the record "succeeds in mixing its safer stylistic choices with its relatively bold ideas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBR_UJ_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-BBR_UJ-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/BBC_Music" title="BBC Music">BBC Music</a>'s Matthew Horton said that the captivating album showcases the singer's knack for songs with chart potential, while noting the incorporation of rock and soul styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horton_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horton-82">[82]</a></sup> Sarah Rodman from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">The Boston Globe</a></i> felt Mars "is trying to rough up his image a bit" as it is undeniable in "his strong, if sometimes oddly lyrically aggressive, second album".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobeReview_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobeReview-35">[35]</a></sup> Ryan Reed, writing in <i><a href="/wiki/Paste_(magazine)" title="Paste (magazine)">Paste</a></i>, gave <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> a 7/10 rating, confessing that "Mars still plays the sweetheart card well, but he's proven himself way more interesting as a badass".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reed-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a mixed review, Caroline Sullivan from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Empire" title="Kitty Empire">Kitty Empire</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i>, and Andrew Chan from <i><a href="/wiki/Slant_Magazine" title="Slant Magazine">Slant Magazine</a></i> all rated <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> three out of five stars. Sullivan dubbed it "the same conventional mish-mash as his 6m-selling debut".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-22">[22]</a></sup> Empire felt that Mars still lacks a characteristic style, observing "a little more hooliganism" than on his first album and feeling that, "despite its title, [it] deserves your grudging respect" while noting the incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Reggae" title="Reggae">reggae</a> on the record. The critic went on arguing that Mars' songwriting values "<a href="/wiki/Story_arc" title="Story arc">narrative arc</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_logic" title="Internal logic">internal logic</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Empire_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Empire-23">[23]</a></sup> Closing the review, Chan called it "not an unqualified triumph, <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> is a step forward" and completed his idea saying that the singer "minor limitation" and "the key to his appeal" on an record that is "a reasonably listenable exercise in genre fetishization."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chan_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-Chan-24">[24]</a></sup> Andy Gill of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> felt that the singer is a "talented chap", but resorts to imitations of past recording artists on the album, "whose title all but gives the game away".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gill-31">[31]</a></sup> Fiona Shepherd of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scotsman" title="The Scotsman">The Scotsman</a></i> wrote that <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> is "a safe mixtape, especially compared to what Mars can pull off live with his terrific soul revue band".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_A.V._Club" title="The A.V. Club">The A.V. Club</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Evan Rytlewsk gave <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> a C+, commenting that Mars is "an undeniable talent, desperately searching for an identity to claim as his own", though praising the tracks "Locked Out Of Heaven", "Natalie", "Treasure" and "Show Me".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rytlewski_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rytlewski-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a>'s Tim Sendra felt the record is "a step back from <i>Doo-Wops &amp; Hooligans</i> in so many ways" and criticized the singer for being an "icky hater", hoping that "Mars can sort out his feelings about women and get back to being a sweet romancer" since his "opinion of the opposite sex seems to have taken a nosedive" after his first album's sentimental lyrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sendra_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accolades">Accolades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Accolades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> received a <a href="/wiki/Juno_Award" title="Juno Award">Juno Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/Juno_Award_for_International_Album_of_the_Year" title="Juno Award for International Album of the Year">International Album of the Year</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Juno_Awards_of_2014" title="Juno Awards of 2014">2014 Juno Awards</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Pop_Vocal_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Pop Vocal Album">Best Pop Vocal Album</a> at the <a href="/wiki/56th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="56th Annual Grammy Awards">56th Annual Grammy Awards</a>. Its lead single, "<a href="/wiki/Locked_Out_of_Heaven" title="Locked Out of Heaven">Locked Out of Heaven</a>", was nominated for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Record_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Record of the Year">Record of the Year</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Song_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Song of the Year">Song of the Year</a>, while its Sultan + Ned Shepard remix received a nomination for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Remixed_Recording,_Non-Classical" title="Grammy Award for Best Remixed Recording, Non-Classical">Best Remixed Recording, Non-Classical</a>. "Locked Out of Heaven" had previously won Best Song at the <a href="/wiki/2013_MTV_Europe_Music_Awards" title="2013 MTV Europe Music Awards">2013 MTV Europe Music Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Afterwards, the record's second release, "<a href="/wiki/When_I_Was_Your_Man" title="When I Was Your Man">When I Was Your Man</a>", earned a nomination for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Pop_Solo_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Pop Solo Performance">Best Pop Solo Performance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grammys_2014_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grammys_2014-86">[86]</a></sup> In December 2013, the album was placed at number two on online music service Rdio's list of "Top global albums"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> and according to <a href="/wiki/Spotify" title="Spotify">Spotify</a>, another online music service, <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was the fifth most-streamed record worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> On a list compiled by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Huffington_Post" title="The Huffington Post">The Huffington Post</a></i> before the 56th Annual Grammy Awards, Michael Glitz considered <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> the 42th best album of 2012 among 50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/HitFix" title="HitFix">HitFix</a> and <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a> created two different lists that featured The 20 Best Albums of 2012, the recording ranked at number 8 and 20, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Suzan Gursoy of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice" title="The Village Voice">The Village Voice</a></i> considered the release the 177th Best Albums of 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_performance">Commercial performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Commercial performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> debuted at number two on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> with first-week sales of 192,000 copies (134,400 physical sales and 57,600 digital sales), topping the expectations of sales that were around 140,000-150,000 copies, earning Bruno Mars his highest peak on the chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB1W_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-BB1W-93">[93]</a></sup> The next week, it sold 178,000 copies, dropping to the third position with an 8% sales decrease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBW2_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBW2-94">[94]</a></sup> The album secured a third consecutive week within the top five in America, withstanding a 38% sales drop to 110,000 copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBW3_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBW3-95">[95]</a></sup> Three weeks after the debut, the record sold a total of 480,000 copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB1W_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-BB1W-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBW2_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBW2-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBW3_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBW3-95">[95]</a></sup> On March 7, 2013, twelve weeks since its availability, the record reached the top spot of the <i>Billboard</i> 200 with 95,000 copies sold, mainly due to an Amazon MP3 sale pricing ($1.99 for a day and $3.99 for the rest of the week).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-BB-96">[96]</a></sup> The album sold 1,399,000 copies in the United States until January 2014, making it the fifth best-selling album of the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sales_2013_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-sales_2013-97">[97]</a></sup> In the week of February 5, 2014, following Mars' presence at the <a href="/wiki/56th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="56th Annual Grammy Awards">56th Annual Grammy Awards</a> and performance at the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XLVIII_halftime_show" title="Super Bowl XLVIII halftime show">Super Bowl XLVIII halftime show</a>, sales for the album increased by 180%, rebounding <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> back in the top ten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBHS1_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-SBHS1-99">[99]</a></sup> For the week ending February 12, 2014, the album climbed to the third position with sales of 81,000, up 92%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBHS2_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-SBHS2-100">[100]</a></sup> making a total of 123,000 units sold in both weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBHS1_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-SBHS1-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBHS2_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-SBHS2-100">[100]</a></sup> The album has been certified four times Platinum by the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a> (RIAA), and as of October 15, 2016, it has sold 2,510,000 total copies in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riaa_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-riaa-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAlbumSales_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-USAlbumSales-102">[102]</a></sup> The record's success in 2013 was decisive to Mars being chosen as the headline performance on the half-time show of the Super Bowl XLVIII, and appearing on <i>Billboard Artist of the Year</i> by <i>Billboard</i>; according to Bill Werde, an editorial director of the magazine, the singer's songs that "stuck around on multiple formats all year round" played a key role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_artist_of_the_year_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_artist_of_the_year-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-103">[103]</a></sup> He was also the most-played artist at Pop Radio (Top 40), the third most played at Rhythmic stations and Hot Adult Contemporary station (HAC), as well as the fifth most-played at AC stations in 2013, according to <a href="/wiki/Mediabase" title="Mediabase">Mediabase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In addition, Mars topped three of Billboard's 2013 year-end charts (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_100" title="Hot 100">Hot 100</a> Artist, <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_Top_40" title="Mainstream Top 40">Mainstream Top 40</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_Digital_Songs" title="Hot Digital Songs">Hot Digital Songs</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Europe, the album was similarly successful. It debuted at number one on the <a href="/wiki/UK_Albums_Chart" title="UK Albums Chart">UK Albums Chart</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Official_Charts_Company" title="Official Charts Company">Official Charts Company</a> predicted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> with 136,391 copies sold in its first week, thus becoming the third fastest-selling album recorded by an artist in 2012, after <a href="/wiki/Mumford_%26_Sons" title="Mumford &amp; Sons">Mumford &amp; Sons</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Babel_(album)" title="Babel (album)">Babel</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Take_Me_Home_(One_Direction_album)" title="Take Me Home (One Direction album)">Take Me Home</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/One_Direction" title="One Direction">One Direction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MusicWeek_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-MusicWeek-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kreisler_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kreisler-106">[106]</a></sup> It first brought total sales to over 100,000 copies in the United Kingdom, thus earning a Gold certification, but was later certified three times Platinum by the <a href="/wiki/British_Phonographic_Industry" title="British Phonographic Industry">British Phonographic Industry</a> (BPI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bpi_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-bpi-107">[107]</a></sup> It has sold over 987,000 copies in the UK as of November 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_sales_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-UK_sales-108">[108]</a></sup> In France, <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> debuted in the top ten and spent the whole year within the top twenty, except for two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_Sales_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-French_Sales-109">[109]</a></sup> One year after its release, album sales exceeded 580,000 copies, therefore being certified Diamond by the <a href="/wiki/Syndicat_National_de_l%27%C3%89dition_Phonographique" title="Syndicat National de l'Édition Phonographique">Syndicat National de l'Édition Phonographique</a> (SNEP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UJCF_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-UJCF-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SNEP_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-SNEP-111">[111]</a></sup> As of 2014, the record sold 580,000 units in that territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_Sales_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-French_Sales-109">[109]</a></sup> In Switzerland, the album debuted atop the charts and was certified Platinum,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFPI_Swi_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-IFPI_Swi-112">[112]</a></sup> while opening at number four on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danish_Albums_Chart" title="Danish Albums Chart">Danish Albums Chart</a> and earning a Gold disc there after five months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFPI_Den_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-IFPI_Den-113">[113]</a></sup> The record further charted within the top ten on the <a href="/wiki/Productores_de_M%C3%BAsica_de_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Productores de Música de España">Spanish Albums Chart</a> for the first time after 30 weeks, the longest time an album needed since American rock band <a href="/wiki/The_Black_Keys" title="The Black Keys">The Black Keys</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/El_Camino_(The_Black_Keys_album)" title="El Camino (The Black Keys album)">El Camino</a></i> (2011) (61 weeks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spanish_Charts_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spanish_Charts-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Oceania, <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> debuted at number nine in New Zealand and was certified Gold by the <a href="/wiki/Recorded_Music_NZ" title="Recorded Music NZ">Recorded Music NZ</a>, selling over 7,500 copies in two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NZChart_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-NZChart-116">[116]</a></sup> The album eventually reached its peak position at number two in that country, marking the singer's consecutive record to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HungMedien_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-HungMedien-117">[117]</a></sup> As of February 2014, <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was certified three times Platinum in New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIANZ_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-RIANZ-118">[118]</a></sup> The record entered the Australian charts at number three, gradually climbing to the top spot, with it bringing total sales to 210,000 units and being similarly certified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HungMedien_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-HungMedien-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARIA_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARIA-119">[119]</a></sup> In Canada, <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> opened the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Albums_Chart" title="Canadian Albums Chart">Canadian Albums Chart</a> at number two, later reaching number one on the week ending July 20, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In Japan, the album debuted at number eleven on the <a href="/wiki/Oricon" title="Oricon">Oricon Albums Chart</a>, with first-week sales counting 18,414 copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> By the end of the year 2013, the record sold a total of 186,825 units, therefore reaching Gold status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPYEC_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPYEC-123">[123]</a></sup> The album topped the <a href="/wiki/Asociaci%C3%B3n_Mexicana_de_Productores_de_Fonogramas_y_Videogramas" title="Asociación Mexicana de Productores de Fonogramas y Videogramas">Mexican Albums Chart</a> and remains the seventh best-selling release there, with it spending 55 weeks in the chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> It also earned a two times Platinum+Gold certification for exceeding sales of over 150,000 copies in that territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMPROFON_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMPROFON-125">[125]</a></sup> As of 2014, Unorthodox Jukebox sold 3.2 million units globally,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> while bringing totals sales to 6 million copies by January 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/Supa_Dups" title="Supa Dups">Dwayne "Supa Dups" Chin Quee</a> – composer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Benny_Blanco" title="Benny Blanco">Benny Blanco</a> – composer</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release_history">Release history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Unorthodox_Jukebox&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Release history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On October 15, 2012, the album was made available for pre-order worldwide via Mars' official site, offering different options to purchase including an immediate MP3 download of "Locked out of Heaven", the standard CD or digital release, the deluxe bundle (which included the record's physical issue, a T-shirt, and an ultimate bundle packaged similarly to the deluxe edition that featured an autographed screen print poster limited for the first 300 orders), and a key necklace for the single.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> The album was officially released on December 7, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanity_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-sanity-188">[188]</a></sup> A year later, on November 5, 2013, the deluxe edition of the record was made available for consumption worldwide with a different cover artwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deluxe_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Deluxe-129">[129]</a></sup> Four days later, a DVD edition that included the music videos for the first three singles of <i>Unorthodox Jukebox</i> was released in Japan along with the deluxe edition of the album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD.2BDVD_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-CD.2BDVD-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
